style="width:224px;max-width:224px"><div class="thumbimage" style="height:125px;overflow:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg"><img alt="Exterior of building" data-file-height="608" data-file-width="1080" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg/222px-Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg/333px-Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg/444px-Chihuahuita_exhibit_at_the_El_Paso_Museum_of_History.jpg 2x" width="222"/></a></div></div></div><div class="trow" style="display:flex"><div class="thumbcaption">Left: "Cabinet of curiosities" style of exhibit, c.1890. Right: Contemporary history exhibit, 2016.</div></div></div></div>
<p>Some museum scholars have even begun to question whether museums truly need artifacts at all. Historian Steven Conn provocatively asks this question, suggesting that there are fewer objects in <i>all</i> museums now, as they have been progressively replaced by interactive technology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> As educational programming has grown in museums, mass collections of objects have receded in importance. This is not necessarily a negative development. <a href="/wiki/Dorothy_Canfield_Fisher" title="Dorothy Canfield Fisher">Dorothy Canfield Fisher</a> observed that the reduction in objects has pushed museums to grow from institutions that artlessly showcased their many artifacts (in the style of early cabinets of curiosity) to instead "thinning out" the objects presented "for a general view of any given subject or period, and to put the rest away in archive-storage-rooms, where they could be consulted by students, the only people who really needed to see them".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> This phenomenon of disappearing objects is especially present in science museums like the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Science_and_Industry_(Chicago)" title="Museum of Science and Industry (Chicago)">Museum of Science and Industry</a> in <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>, which have a high visitorship of school-aged children who may benefit more from hands-on interactive technology than reading a label beside an artifact.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types">Types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:National_mall_(east)_satellite_image.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="184" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="81" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg/350px-National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg/525px-National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg/700px-National_mall_%28east%29_satellite_image.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:National_mall_(east)_satellite_image.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The National Mall in Washington DC is home to variety of museum types.</div></div></div>
<p>There is no definitive standard as to the set types of museums. Additionally, the museum landscape has become so varied, that it may not be sufficient to use traditional categories to comprehend fully the vast variety existing throughout the world. However, it may be useful to categorize museums in different ways under multiple perspectives. Museums can vary based on size, from large institutions, to very small institutions focusing on specific subjects, such as a specific location, a notable person, or a given period of time. Museums also can be based on the main source of funding: central or federal government, provinces, regions, universities; towns and communities; other subsidised; nonsubsidised and private.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>It may sometimes be useful to distinguish between <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/diachronic" title="wikt:diachronic">diachronic</a> museums - those that interpret the way in which its subject matter has developed and evolved through time (examples: <a href="/wiki/Lower_East_Side_Tenement_Museum" title="Lower East Side Tenement Museum">Lower East Side Tenement Museum</a> and <a href="/wiki/Diachronic_Museum_of_Larissa" title="Diachronic Museum of Larissa">Diachronic Museum of Larissa</a>), and <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/synchronic" title="wikt:synchronic">synchronic</a> museums - those that interpret the way in which its subject matter exists at one point in time (examples:The <a href="/wiki/Anne_Frank_House" title="Anne Frank House">Anne Frank House</a> and <a href="/wiki/Colonial_Williamsburg" title="Colonial Williamsburg">Colonial Williamsburg</a>). According to University of Florida's Professor Eric Kilgerman, "While a museum in which a particular narrative unfolds within its halls is diachronic, those museums that limit their space to a single experience are called synchronic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>In her book, Civilizing the Museum, author Elaine Heumann Gurian proposes that there are five categories of museums based on intention not content. Object Centered, Narrative, Client Centered, Community Centered, and National.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Museums can also be categorized into major groups by the type of collections they display, to include: <a href="/wiki/Fine_art" title="Fine art">fine arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Applied_arts" title="Applied arts">applied arts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Craft" title="Craft">craft</a>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeology" title="Archaeology">archaeology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">anthropology</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethnology" title="Ethnology">ethnology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biography" title="Biography">biography</a>, <a href="/wiki/History" title="History">history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cultural_history" title="Cultural history">cultural history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_museum" title="Children's museum">children's museums</a>, <a href="/wiki/Natural_history" title="Natural history">natural history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Botanical_garden" title="Botanical garden">botanical</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zoo" title="Zoo">zoological gardens</a>. Within these categories, many museums specialize further, e.g. museums of <a href="/wiki/Modern_art" title="Modern art">modern art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Folk_art" title="Folk art">folk art</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_history" title="Local history">local history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Military_history" title="Military history">military history</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_aviation" title="History of aviation">aviation history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philately" title="Philately">philately</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Geology" title="Geology">geology</a>. The size of a museum's collection typically determines the museum's size, whereas its collection reflects the type of museum it is. Many museums normally display a "permanent collection" of important selected objects in its area of specialization, and may periodically display "special collections" on a temporary basis.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Major_museum_types">Major museum types</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Major museum types">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following is a list to give an idea of the major museum types. While comprehensive it is not a definitive list. 
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 20em;">

</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Legal_framework_of_museums">Legal framework of museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legal framework of museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_vs._private_museums">Public vs. private museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Public vs. private museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Private museums are organized by individuals and managed by a board and museum officers, but public museums are created and managed by federal, state, or local governments. A government can charter a museum through legislative action but the museum can still be private as it is not part of the government. The distinction regulates the ownership and legal accountability for the care of the collections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foundations_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Foundations-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegalPrimer_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-LegalPrimer-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-profit_vs._for-profit_museums">Non-profit vs. for-profit museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Non-profit vs. for-profit museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Nonprofit means that an organization is classified as a charitable corporation and is exempt from paying most taxes and the money the organization earns is invested in the organization itself. Money made by a private, for-profit museum is paid to the museum's owners or shareholders.
</p><p>The nonprofit museum has a fiduciary responsibility in regards to the public, in essence the museum holds its collections and administers it for the benefit of the public. Collections of for-profit museums are legally corporate assets the museum administers for the benefit of the owners or shareholders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Foundations_38-2"><a href="#cite_note-Foundations-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LegalPrimer_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-LegalPrimer-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Museums_run_by_trusts_vs._corporations">Museums run by trusts vs. corporations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Museums run by trusts vs. corporations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>A trust is a legal instrument where trustees manage the trust's assets for the benefit of the museum following the specific wishes of the donor. This provides tax benefits for the donor, and also allows the donor to have control over how assets are distributed.
</p><p>Corporations are legal entities and may acquire property in a way similar to how an individual can own property. Museums under incorporation are usually organized by a community or group of individuals. While  a board of director's loyalty is to the corporation, a board of trustee's loyalty has to be loyal to the intention of the trust. The ramification is that a trust is far less flexible than a corporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Current_challenges_facing_museums">Current challenges facing museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Current challenges facing museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decolonization_of_museums">Decolonization of museums</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Decolonization of museums">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decolonisation_of_knowledge" title="Decolonisation of knowledge">Decolonisation of knowledge</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basalt_statue,_Hoa_Hakananai%27a_(hidden_or_stolen_friend)._Moai;_an_ancestor_figure,_made_by_the_Rapanui_people._1000-1200_CE._From_Orongo_(Polynesia,_Easter_Island);_probably_made_in_Rano_Kao._British_Museum.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="6016" data-file-width="4016" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Basalt_statue%2C_Hoa_Hakananai%27a_%28hidden_or_stolen_friend%29._Moai%3B_an_ancestor_figure%2C_made_by_the_Rapanui_people._1000-1200_CE._From_Orongo_%28Polynesia%2C_Easter_Island%29%3B_probably_made_in_Rano_Kao._British_Museum.jpg/200px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Basalt_statue%2C_Hoa_Hakananai%27a_%28hidden_or_stolen_friend%29._Moai%3B_an_ancestor_figure%2C_made_by_the_Rapanui_people._1000-1200_CE._From_Orongo_%28Polynesia%2C_Easter_Island%29%3B_probably_made_in_Rano_Kao._British_Museum.jpg/300px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Basalt_statue%2C_Hoa_Hakananai%27a_%28hidden_or_stolen_friend%29._Moai%3B_an_ancestor_figure%2C_made_by_the_Rapanui_people._1000-1200_CE._From_Orongo_%28Polynesia%2C_Easter_Island%29%3B_probably_made_in_Rano_Kao._British_Museum.jpg/400px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basalt_statue,_Hoa_Hakananai%27a_(hidden_or_stolen_friend)._Moai;_an_ancestor_figure,_made_by_the_Rapanui_people._1000-1200_CE._From_Orongo_(Polynesia,_Easter_Island);_probably_made_in_Rano_Kao._British_Museum.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Moai figure at the British Museum</div></div></div><p>During the beginning of the 21st century, a growing global movement for the decolonization of museums has arisen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Proponent" title="Proponent">Proponents</a> of this movement argue that 'museums are a box of things' and do not represent complete stories; instead they show biased narratives based on ideologies, in which certain stories are intentionally disregarded.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_49-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9–18">: 9–18 </span></sup> Through this, people are encouraging others to consider this missing perspective, when looking at museum collections, as every object viewed in such environments was placed by an individual to represent a certain viewpoint, be it historical or cultural.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_49-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 9–18">: 9–18 </span></sup>
</p><p>The 2018 <a href="/wiki/Report_on_the_restitution_of_African_cultural_heritage" title="Report on the restitution of African cultural heritage"><i>report on</i> <i>the restitution of African cultural heritage</i></a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rapport_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rapport-74">[74]</a></sup> is a prominent example regarding the decolonization of museums and other collections in France and the claims of African countries to regain <a href="/wiki/Artifact_(archaeology)" title="Artifact (archaeology)">artifacts</a> illegally taken from their original cultural settings.
</p><p>Since 1868, several monolithic human figures known as <a href="/wiki/Moai" title="Moai">Moai</a> have been <a href="/wiki/Relocation_of_moai" title="Relocation of moai">removed</a> from <a href="/wiki/Easter_Island" title="Easter Island">Easter Island</a> and put in display in major Western museums such as the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_of_Natural_History" title="National Museum of Natural History">National Museum of Natural History</a>, the British Museum, the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Museums_of_Art_and_History" title="Royal Museums of Art and History">Royal Museums of Art and History</a>. Several demands have been made by Easter Island residents for the return of the Moai.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> The figures are seen as ancestors and family or the soul by the <a href="/wiki/Rapa_Nui_people" title="Rapa Nui people">Rapa Nui</a> and hold deep cultural value to their people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Other examples include the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gweagal_Shield" title="Gweagal Shield">Gweagal Shield</a>, thought to be a very significant shield taken from <a href="/wiki/Botany_Bay" title="Botany Bay">Botany Bay</a> in April 1770<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> or the <a href="/wiki/Elgin_Marbles" title="Elgin Marbles">Parthenon marble</a> sculptures, which were taken from Greece by Lord Elgin in 1805.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-78">[78]</a></sup> Successive Greek governments have unsuccessfully petitioned for the return of the Parthenon marbles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-78">[78]</a></sup> Another example among many others is the so-called <a href="/wiki/Montezuma%27s_headdress" title="Montezuma's headdress">Montezuma's headdress</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Museum_of_Ethnology,_Vienna" title="Museum of Ethnology, Vienna">Museum of Ethnology, Vienna</a>, which  is a source of dispute between Austria and Mexico.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-79">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Laura_Van_Broekhoven&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Laura Van Broekhoven (page does not exist)">Laura Van Broekhoven</a>, director of the <a href="/wiki/Pitt_Rivers_Museum" title="Pitt Rivers Museum">Pitt Rivers Museum</a> in Oxford, United Kingdom, stated in 2020 that "ethnographic museums should redress their <a href="/wiki/Coloniality_of_knowledge" title="Coloniality of knowledge">coloniality</a>. They should be a pluriverse that shows the rich diversity of ways of being and knowing, not centering <a href="/wiki/Whiteness_theory" title="Whiteness theory">whiteness</a> as the only way of being. Museums ought to allow for everyone to understand each other better."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Labor_issues_and_Unionization">Labor issues and Unionization</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Labor issues and Unionization">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1,_2022-001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3400" data-file-width="4803" decoding="async" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg/220px-Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg/330px-Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg/440px-Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1%2C_2022-001.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Art_Museum_workers_rally_-_April_1,_2022-001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Workers rallying at the Philadelphia Museum of Art</div></div></div>
<dl><dt>Background</dt></dl>
<p>The past few years has seen a unionizing movement. US museums workers have initiated dialogs about labor and collective organizing in the cultural sector. In 2019 the workers in multiple museums voted to form unions with more protesting to press for a fair contract and against unfair labor practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> During that year over 3,000 cultural workers anonymously started to share their salaries online through an pay transparency spreadsheet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-Small-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Marciano Art Foundation, a museum established by co-founders of <a href="/wiki/Guess_(clothing)" title="Guess (clothing)">Guess clothing</a>, Maurice Marciano and <a href="/wiki/Paul_Marciano" title="Paul Marciano">Paul Marciano</a> closed indefinitely in November 2019 after workers attempted to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unionize" title="Unionize">unionize</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The Marciano Foundation released a statement a month later that the closure was permanent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the country of <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(country)" title="Georgia (country)">Georgia</a> 40 employees were fired May 2022 as part of a restructuring. The newly formed union, the Georgian Trade Union of Science, Education, and Culture Workers said in a statement they said the employees were fired illegally and the reorganization was "carried out by the employer in an untransparent and maladministered manner" and that the organization will "definitely fight to the end to protect the rights of employees." Fired senior curator Maia Pataridze said the new management mentioned her social media posts criticizing the government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> Among those fired was union chair, Nikoloz Tsikaridze, a senior researcher and archaeologist who associated the discharging of himself and other museum staff was for forming a union, and said that <a href="/wiki/Thea_Tsulukiani" title="Thea Tsulukiani">Thea Tsulukiani</a>, the Georgia Minister of Culture had ‘punished’ them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<dl><dt>History</dt></dl>
<p>In the United States, labor unrest within the arts and cultural sector go back at lest nearly a century to 1933 when a New York based collective of artists eventually known as the Artist’s Union used collective bargaining for state relief for unemployed artists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1971 administrative staff at New York’s Museum of Modern Art formed the organization "Professional and Staff Association of the Museum of Modern Art" (PASTA), the first union of professional employees, as opposed to maintenance and service people, at a privately‐financed museum. The contract negotiated would provide a wage increase, protection against termination without cause, and direct access to trustees and policy-making processes at the museum. While there was some interest from workers at other museums at the time, for the next fifty years there was little change in museums adding union representation of their professional employees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Small_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-Small-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainability_and_climate_change">Sustainability and climate change</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Museum&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Sustainability and climate change">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Green_Museum" title="Green Museum">Green Museum</a></div>
<p>Increasingly museums have been responded to the ongoing climate crisis through enacting sustainable museum practices, and exhibitions highlighting the issues surrounding <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anthropocene" title="Anthropocene">Anthropocene</a>.
</p>
